<div id="review_181316368" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/181316368" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4950364-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1297406196p2/4950364.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/181316368?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/4950364-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181316368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11286572514135328402">Once again, I find that several of the interesting ideas percolating through my own brain have already been developed (and published) by some famous author a long time ago. At least David Brin had the courtesy to only beat me to print by a couple of decades, unlike those damned Pre-Socratics...<br/><br/>But in all seriousness, this is a great book, and it has weathered the last twenty years far better than its ilk usually have any right to expect. It's a sweeping, grand, thrilling story that starts out as</span>
  <span id="freeText11286572514135328402" style="display:none">Once again, I find that several of the interesting ideas percolating through my own brain have already been developed (and published) by some famous author a long time ago. At least David Brin had the courtesy to only beat me to print by a couple of decades, unlike those damned Pre-Socratics...<br /><br />But in all seriousness, this is a great book, and it has weathered the last twenty years far better than its ilk usually have any right to expect. It's a sweeping, grand, thrilling story that starts out as hard sci-fi and ends up as...something else. But along the way, Brin shows us a world thirty years in the future (fifty when he was writing) that is eerily familiar in many ways. While his predictions miss the mark as often as they hit it square on, his success rate is nevertheless astounding. But the real reason to read this book (aside from the fact that it's great fun and full of global warming, earthquakes, black holes, tactical geology, radical philosophy, cyber warfare, space ships and lasers) is that many of the themes and issues he explores are just as relevant today as they were in 1989. <br /><br />It's got its flaws (notably a detectably pedantic tone at times) but it's definitely worth the read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11286572514135328402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14532646596621089595" style="display:none">This one was a book group read. I picked it up on Sunday morning, saw it was 600 pages and cursed. I flew threw it and finished it by Thursday. I can say I didn't actually hate it until the last 100 pages or so.<br /><br />Quick spoiler filled recap. It's the near future and earth has evolved into a hot, overpopulated crapsack world. Then one of our main characters through some fantasy physics creates a black hole and drops it into Earths core. So far so good. Then we discover the other black hole put there by aliens. Oh, and the main religion on Earth now is Gaia worship. And there is now no privacy. And the Swiss are the bad guys. I actually liked that.<br /><br />The real problem with the book is the insane ending with the gravity beams, crazy ecco-terrorists, and above all the whole assention of one of the characters to godhood. Oh, let's not forget the nice aliens.<br /><br />A lot of that would be forgiven if the prose and story were right, but this book cries out for a good editor. It meanders, digressions and bores. A lot of it is just pointless masturbation by the author. To emphasise the point he includes an afterward lecture at the end to hammer this home. I think we get the point. </span>

Be patient with this book because the real story starts towards the end.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8232298147935121058">This has a very solid collection of hard sci-fi concepts, all thrown into one place. But it still works, it's a solid work of sci-fi, with p</span>
  <span id="freeText8232298147935121058" style="display:none">This has a very solid collection of hard sci-fi concepts, all thrown into one place. I enjoyed it quite a lot, I cared for the characters and felt intrigued the whole time, though at some point there's more and more new concepts being thrown at you that they hit a bit too hard and becomes unbelievable.<br /><br />It feels as if the plot was constructed backwards to justify the arrival at one specific event, and in the process it became too far fetched. But it still works, it's a solid work of sci-fi, with plenty of local flavors and world building that add a great dimension to it.</span>

<div id="review_863930438" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/863930438" />
    <a title="Todd Martin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1345046-todd-martin"><img alt="Todd Martin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442610267p2/1345046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/863930438?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Todd Martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Todd Martin" href="/user/show/1345046-todd-martin">Todd Martin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1345046-todd-martin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer863930438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12337892581728702194">A few years ago I read David Brin’s <i>The Postman</i> and was rather unimpressed by the silly plot-line and story. Then, a few months ago, I got a chance to hear Brin speak and found his talk quite interesting. It was clear that he had thought quite a bit about the near future (next 50 years) and possible scenarios that may arise given a continuation of current trends. His discussion touched on the future of the security state, animal rights, evolution and technology as well as the societal issues tha</span>
  <span id="freeText12337892581728702194" style="display:none">A few years ago I read David Brin’s <i>The Postman</i> and was rather unimpressed by the silly plot-line and story. Then, a few months ago, I got a chance to hear Brin speak and found his talk quite interesting. It was clear that he had thought quite a bit about the near future (next 50 years) and possible scenarios that may arise given a continuation of current trends. His discussion touched on the future of the security state, animal rights, evolution and technology as well as the societal issues that may accompany these changes. <br /><br />With that as a backdrop, I figured I’d give Brin another chance … which brings us to <i>Earth</i>. The book is set in 2038 on an ecologically devastated planet. The ozone layer has been depleted and global warming has resulted in climatic shifts that have resulted in the massive die-off of species and flooding of coastal areas. Add to this the unhappy fact that a scientific experiment gone awry has caused a tiny black hole to drop into the center of the Earth threatening the entire planet. Lest you think this is a dystopian tale, scientific progress has made significant advances and civilization is thriving despite the challenges in feeding a global population of 10 million.<br /><br />The book includes quite a bit of real science (Brin has a PhD in space science), while the fictional elements of the story have a scientific foundation at their base lending a sense of realism to the tale (it doesn’t hurt that many of the elements in the book, which was written in 1990, have come true in the intervening decades). In addition, Brin does a nice job depicting the sociological response of the planet’s citizenry to these events. In particular, I liked the his descriptions of subgroups that form around various societal issues … for example; Gaia veneration, a sun cult, the merging of deep cultural myth with hard physics, etc. <br /><br />All this is to say that the story is a good one, and has redeemed Brin in my eyes and will have to read more of his work in … the future. <br /><br />By way of minor criticism I will say that none of Brin’s characters have much in the way of distinct personalities and that the plot, though interesting, doesn’t pack much in the way of action until towards the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12337892581728702194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1021376563" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1021376563" />
    <a title="Atheshya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33797668-atheshya"><img alt="Atheshya" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1021376563?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Atheshya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Atheshya" href="/user/show/33797668-atheshya">Atheshya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780553057782" href="/book/show/545929.Earth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33797668-atheshya?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1021376563" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17816433631304852685">There's a lot of things about this book that make it, frankly, awesome. The first is probably the most obvious: it's written in some attempt to predict the future, or to at least be close to the ball park of what the future might be. And from my vantage point of about 20 years after it was written, an amazing amount turns out to seem pretty familiar. While we haven't yet had a literal war with banks and bankers in the real world, for instance, that sure reminds me of some things that have been i</span>
  <span id="freeText17816433631304852685" style="display:none">There's a lot of things about this book that make it, frankly, awesome. The first is probably the most obvious: it's written in some attempt to predict the future, or to at least be close to the ball park of what the future might be. And from my vantage point of about 20 years after it was written, an amazing amount turns out to seem pretty familiar. While we haven't yet had a literal war with banks and bankers in the real world, for instance, that sure reminds me of some things that have been in the cultural air the past few years.<br /><br />There's also the fact that the characters are mostly really good. Each are headed with the name of a part of Earth, too - like the crust or the hydrosphere. There's a few who I never ended up caring about, like the guy associated with the mantle. I apparently don't even remember his name. But still, most were good.<br /><br />The pacing is also generally very cinematic, with kind of epic reveals and cliffhangers. It also turns out that apparently the way to do exposition correctly is to hint at something until the reader is curious enough to <i>want</i> to read several paragraphs of pages of description about what it is, and then give them that. <br /><br />And then there's the ending. Jesus Christ, the ending. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I don't think I've ever seen a twist done quite that well. It turns out that one of the reasons that the book is called Earth is that one of the characters LITERALLY BECOMES MOTHER EARTH, basically a god living somehow in the flow of metals in the core. And yet it comes across as a fairly reasonable occurrence in the context of the buildup for it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I will be sure to reread this sometime (and definitely on the year it takes place in), since there's a lot of detail to this, and all the detail is good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17816433631304852685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CnRTRtJ1FJJ3U+Ybcod9GZvu6pGPukmXN1dqkjJNecUtUzYbLxEYkbxY1zen8v0WemvOgKde63qwPtxQDQpsFQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VaV+aZU/QFxmG81sPHQyugfcsB2kyETprNhygCItht1yghs0aFtMX60Q/EDpAbK15lmUDIws5gQrscRCHWqTDQ==')})
  }

His battle to realize his dream results in two momentous discoveries anti-gravity and the secret of immortality. In A Life for the Stars, it is centuries later and anti-gravity generations have enabled whole cities to lift off the surface of the earth to become galactic wanderers. In Earthman, Come Home, the nomadic cities revert to barbarism and marauding rogue cities begin to pose a threat to all civilized worlds. In the final novel, The Triumph of Time, history repeats itself as the cities once again journey back in to space making a terrifying discovery which could destroy the entire Universe. They have to do that in ways that are very diffe<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17339348825893118554\" style=\"display:none\">There are some incredibly smart things Brunner does in this novel. The story is told from the perspective of a world of intelligent aliens as they reach out to discover the universe in which they live. They have to do that in ways that are very different from our own history in details (for example, they live under water where access to the night sky is limited, which puts a crimp in early astronomy), but very similar in the abstract. The similarities arise for the simple reason that the universe in which they live is THE universe. The message here is deep &amp; subtle &amp; important: reality is what it is, &amp; no matter what kind of body you have, no matter what specific environmental niche you occupy, if you are smart enough to wonder about the world you live in, &amp; clever enough to discover ways to ask your questions well, you will discover the same immutable facts about the nature of things. Brunner shows this without ever giving a lecture or explicitly making the point.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/14078.David_Brin">About David Brin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/14078.David_Brin"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1352956147p3/14078.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/14078.David_Brin">David Brin
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6963987409160263882">David Brin is a scientist, speaker, and world-known author. His novels have been New York Times Bestsellers, winning multiple Hugo, Nebula and other awards. At least a dozen have been translated into more than twenty languages.<br/><br/> Existence, his latest novel, offers an unusual scenario for first contact. His ecological thriller, Earth, foreshadowed global warming, cyberwarfare and near-future trends </span>
  <span id="freeText6963987409160263882" style="display:none">David Brin is a scientist, speaker, and world-known author. His novels have been New York Times Bestsellers, winning multiple Hugo, Nebula and other awards. At least a dozen have been translated into more than twenty languages.<br /><br /> Existence, his latest novel, offers an unusual scenario for first contact. His ecological thriller, Earth, foreshadowed global warming, cyberwarfare and near-future trends such as the World Wide Web. A movie, directed by Kevin Costner, was loosely based on his post-apocalyptic novel, The Postman. Startide Rising won the Hugo and Nebula Awards for best novel. The Uplift War also won the Hugo Award.<br /><br /> His non-fiction book -- The Transparent Society: Will Technology Force Us to Choose Between Freedom and Privacy? -- deals with secrecy in the modern world. It won the Freedom of Speech Prize from the American Library Association.<br /><br /> Brin serves on advisory committees dealing with subjects as diverse as national defense and homeland security, astronomy and space exploration, SETI, nanotechnology, and philanthropy.<br /><br /> David appears frequently on TV, including "The Universe" and on the History Channel's "Life After People."<br /><br /> Full and updated at:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.davidbrin.com/biography.htm">http://www.davidbrin.com/biography.htm</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6963987409160263882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
